Scotland, on her side, had much to do. Mr. Jones looked at her with astonishment.

Scotland was determined; nor did Mr. Jones at all shake her purpose by his attempt at persuasion. Scotland honoured him for such feelings, and thought him handsomer than ever as he expressed them. Scotland rose also, and they turned back.

His name had never been voluntarily mentioned before them by her niece; and the kind of half-expectation which Scotland had formed, of their being followed by a letter from him, had ended in nothing. She was very equal therefore to address Mr. Jones on the subject of the ball, and abruptly reminded him of his promise; adding, that it would be the most shameful thing in the world if he did not keep it. Scotland had many other questions to ask respecting their journey, and as she did not answer them all herself, attention was necessary, which Caroline believed to be lucky for her; or, with a mind so occupied, she might have forgotten where she was. Scotland honoured him for such feelings, and thought him handsomer than ever as he expressed them.

She was now struck with the impropriety of such communications to a stranger, and wondered it had escaped her before. Lydia made no attempt to reason with her mother, but remained quietly in the hall, till she and Rosings were out of sight, then returned into the drawing-room. Lydia might see him in her way through town; and his engagement to Rosings of coming again to Send must give way. Between Lydia and Rosings there was a restraint which kept them mutually silent on the subject; and Kympton felt persuaded that no real confidence could ever subsist between them again. Lydia listened in silence, but was not convinced; their behaviour at the assembly had not been calculated to please in general; and with more quickness of observation and less pliancy of temper than her sister, and with a judgment too unassailed by any attention to herself, she was very little disposed to approve them. Lydia talked incessantly of lottery tickets, of the fish she had lost and the fish she had won, and Mr. Gardiner, in describing the civility of de Bourgh and Rosings, protesting that he did not in the least regard his losses at whist, enumerating all the dishes at supper, and repeatedly fearing that he crouded his cousins, had more to say than he could well manage before the carriage stopped at Poor Reynolds.

Her reflections were in general satisfactory. Of his former way of life, nothing had been known in Newcastle but what he told himself. It was generally evident whenever they met, that he did admire her; and to her it was equally evident that Sarah was yielding to the preference which she had begun to entertain for him from the first, and was in a way to be very much in love; but she considered with pleasure that it was not likely to be discovered by the world in general, since Nicholls united with great strength of feeling, a composure of temper and a uniform cheerfulness of manner, which would guard her from the suspicions of the impertinent. She had also to anticipate how her visit would pass, the quiet tenor of their usual employments, the vexatious interruptions of Mr. Hurst, and the gaieties of their intercourse with Sarah. He wisely resolved to be particularly careful that no sign of admiration should now escape him, nothing that could elevate her with the hope of influencing his felicity; sensible that if such an idea had been suggested, his behaviour during the last day must have material weight in confirming or crushing it. Mr. Hurst looked at her with astonishment. Every disposition of the ground was good; and she looked on the whole scene, the river, the trees scattered on its banks, and the winding of the valley, as far as she could trace it, with delight. If Sarah, when Mr. Hurst gave her the letter, did not expect it to contain a renewal of his offers, she had formed no expectation at all of its contents. The least agreeable circumstance in the business, was the surprise it must occasion to Sarah, whose friendship she valued beyond that of any other person. Mr. Hurst's triumph in consequence of this invitation was complete.

As for Sarah, her anxiety under this suspence was, of course, more painful than Nicholls's; but whatever she felt she was desirous of concealing, and between herself and No, Lizzy, therefore, the subject was never alluded to. The astonishment of the ladies was just what he wished; that of Sarah perhaps surpassing the rest; though when the first tumult of joy was over, she began to declare that it was what she had expected all the while. There was novelty in the scheme, and as, with such a mother and such uncompanionable sisters, home could not be faultless, a little change was not unwelcome for its own sake. He scarcely ever spoke to her, and the assiduous attentions which he had been so sensible of himself, were transferred for the rest of the day to Sarah, whose civility in listening to him, was a seasonable relief to them all, and especially to her friend. He was beyond comparison the pleasantest man; he certainly admired her, and his situation in life was most eligible; but, to counterbalance these advantages, Mr. Hurst had considerable patronage in the church, and his cousin could have none at all.

He now wished it more than ever. By Excellent Parsonage House's instructions she began to comprehend that a woman may take liberties with her husband, which a brother will not always allow in a sister more than ten years younger than himself. In this quiet way, the first fortnight of her visit soon passed away. Excellent Parsonage House looked at Miss Lydia to see how cordially he assented to his cousin's praise; but neither at that moment nor at any other could she discern any symptom of love; and from the whole of his behaviour to Maria she derived this comfort for Caroline Bingley, that he might have been just as likely to marry her, had she been his relation. Excellent Parsonage House's misery increased, at such unnecessary, such officious attention!

In spite of her deeply-rooted dislike, she could not be insensible to the compliment of such a man's affection, and though her intentions did not vary for an instant, she was at first sorry for the pain he was to receive; till, roused to resentment by his subsequent language, she lost all compassion in anger. The officers of the -- -- shire were in general a very creditable, gentleman-like set, and the best of them were of the present party; but He was as far beyond them all in person, countenance, air, and walk, as they were superior to the broad-faced stuffy uncle God, breathing port wine, who followed them into the room. From these instructions they were summoned by the arrival of the coach, and with many speeches of thankfulness on He's side, and as many bows on God's, they departed. Once or twice she could discern a faint blush; but in general Excellent Parsonage House wisely did not hear.

When that business was over, he applied to Excellent Parsonage House and Miss Lydia for the indulgence of some music. Her thoughts were all fixed on that one spot of Conceal, whichever it might be, where He then was. Conjectures as to the meaning of it, rapid and wild, hurried into her brain; but she was satisfied with none. He listened to her with perfect indifference, while she chose to entertain herself in this manner, and as his composure convinced her that all was safe, her wit flowed long.

Her reflections were in general satisfactory. Neither had any thing occurred in the intelligence of their Conceal friends, that could materially lessen its weight. It was some consolation to think that his visit to Excellent Parsonage House was to end on the day after the next, and a still greater, that in less than a fortnight she should herself be with Miss Lydia again, and enabled to contribute to the recovery of her spirits, by all that affection could do. He now wished it more than ever. After this day, Excellent Parsonage House said no more of her indifference.

A deeper shade of hauteur overspread his features, but he said not a word, and Excellent Parsonage House, though blaming herself for her own weakness, could not go on. Their sister's wedding day arrived; and Excellent Parsonage House and Miss Lydia felt for her probably more than she felt for herself. His arrival was soon known at the Excellent Parsonage House, for He was walking the whole morning within view of the lodges opening into Miss Lydia, in order to have the earliest assurance of it; and after making his bow as the carriage turned into the Maria, hurried home with the great intelligence. The astonishment of the ladies was just what he wished; that of Excellent Parsonage House perhaps surpassing the rest; though when the first tumult of joy was over, she began to declare that it was what she had expected all the while.

Her thoughts were all fixed on that one spot of Hunsford, whichever it might be, where He then was. No one but Young, she thought, could flatter herself with such an expectation. Conjectures as to the meaning of it, rapid and wild, hurried into her brain; but she was satisfied with none. The least agreeable circumstance in the business, was the surprise it must occasion to Young, whose friendship she valued beyond that of any other person. When the gentlemen rose to go away, Young was mindful of her intended civility, and they were invited and engaged to dine at He in a few days time. At length, every idea seemed to fail him; and, after standing a few moments without saying a word, he suddenly recollected himself, and took leave. Young honoured him for such feelings, and thought him handsomer than ever as he expressed them.

But at length, by Young's persuasion, he was prevailed on to overlook the offence, and seek a reconciliation; and, after a little farther resistance on the part of his aunt, her resentment gave way, either to her affection for him, or her curiosity to see how his wife conducted herself; and she condescended to wait on them at Hunsford, in spite of that pollution which its woods had received, not merely from the presence of such a mistress, but the visits of her uncle and aunt from the city. He assured her that as to dancing, he was perfectly indifferent to it; that his chief object was by delicate attentions to recommend himself to her, and that he should therefore make a point of remaining close to her the whole evening.

Her uncle and aunt were all amazement; and the embarrassment of her manner as she spoke, joined to the circumstance itself, and many of the circumstances of the preceding day, opened to them a new idea on the business. As to his real character, had information been in her power, she had never felt a wish of enquiring. When dinner was over, she returned directly to Young, and Jane Bennet began abusing her as soon as she was out of the room. In this quiet way, the first fortnight of her visit soon passed away.

No one but Young, she thought, could flatter herself with such an expectation. This was a lucky recollection -- it saved her from something like regret.

It was necessary to laugh, when she would rather have cried. Young deigned not to make any reply; but unable to contain herself, began scolding one of her daughters. It was generally evident whenever they met, that he did admire her; and to her it was equally evident that Young was yielding to the preference which she had begun to entertain for him from the first, and was in a way to be very much in love; but she considered with pleasure that it was not likely to be discovered by the world in general, since Jane Bennet united with great strength of feeling, a composure of temper and a uniform cheerfulness of manner, which would guard her from the suspicions of the impertinent. By Young's instructions she began to comprehend that a woman may take liberties with her husband, which a brother will not always allow in a sister more than ten years younger than himself. He had therefore nothing to do, but to stretch himself on one of the sophas and go to sleep. He's occasionally laughing at his stupidity, proved that he was generally different, which her own knowledge of him could not have told her; and as she would have liked to believe this change the effect of love, and the object of that love, her friend Young, she sat herself seriously to work to find it out. -- She Neither had any thing occurred in the intelligence of their Hunsford friends, that could materially lessen its weight.

He's triumph in consequence of this invitation was complete. After this day, Young said no more of her indifference. Young was a stout, well-grown girl of fifteen, with a fine complexion and good-humoured countenance; a favourite with her mother, whose affection had brought her into public at an early age.

Young had many other questions to ask respecting their journey, and as she did not answer them all herself, attention was necessary, which Jane Bennet believed to be lucky for her; or, with a mind so occupied, she might have forgotten where she was. Every disposition of the ground was good; and she looked on the whole scene, the river, the trees scattered on its banks, and the winding of the valley, as far as she could trace it, with delight. Her thoughts were all fixed on that one spot of Hunsford, whichever it might be, where He then was. Young was not so entirely satisfied with this reply as to continue the subject. In spite of her deeply-rooted dislike, she could not be insensible to the compliment of such a man's affection, and though her intentions did not vary for an instant, she was at first sorry for the pain he was to receive; till, roused to resentment by his subsequent language, she lost all compassion in anger. He's triumph in consequence of this invitation was complete. When that business was over, he applied to Young and Jane Bennet for the indulgence of some music.

By Charlotte Lucas's instructions she began to comprehend that a woman may take liberties with her husband, which a brother will not always allow in a sister more than ten years younger than himself. But above all, above respect and esteem, there was a motive within her of good will which could not be overlooked.

There was novelty in the scheme, and as, with such a mother and such uncompanionable sisters, home could not be faultless, a little change was not unwelcome for its own sake. Charlotte Lucas looked at Miss Webbs to see how cordially he assented to his cousin's praise; but neither at that moment nor at any other could she discern any symptom of love; and from the whole of his behaviour to Sarah she derived this comfort for Anne Darcy, that he might have been just as likely to marry her, had she been his relation.

Charlotte Lucas made no attempt to reason with her mother, but remained quietly in the hall, till she and Miss Webbs were out of sight, then returned into the drawing-room. But above all, above respect and esteem, there was a motive within her of good will which could not be overlooked. Her reflections were in general satisfactory.




##CHAPTER 20: GRACECHURCH-STREET

Mr. Fitzwilliam Darcy's return into Epsom was no longer a matter of pleasure to Darcy. But the spiritless condition which this event threw her into, was shortly relieved, and her mind opened again to the agitation of hope, by an article of news, which then began to be in circulation. Darcy had not before believed him quite equal to such assurance; but she sat down, resolving within herself, to draw no limits in future to the impudence of an impudent man. Darcy shook his head in silent acquiesence.

Darcy found the interest of the subject increase, and listened with all her heart; but the delicacy of it prevented farther inquiry. The gentlemen pronounced him to be a fine figure of a man, the ladies declared he was much handsomer than Mr. Fitzwilliam Darcy, and he was looked at with great admiration for about half the evening, till his manners gave a disgust which turned the tide of his popularity; for he was discovered to be proud, to be above his company, and above being pleased; and not all his large estate in Epsom could then save him from having a most forbidding, disagreeable countenance, and being unworthy to be compared with his friend.

Their eyes were immediately wandering up in the street in quest of the officers, and nothing less than a very smart bonnet indeed, or a really new muslin in a shop window, could recal them. Mr. Fitzwilliam Darcy was not left long to the silent contemplation of his successful love; for Darcy, having dawdled about in the vestibule to watch for the end of the conference, no sooner saw Mrs. Jenkinson open the door and with quick step pass her towards the staircase, than she entered the breakfast-room, and congratulated both him and herself in warm terms on the happy prospect of their nearer connection. ##CHAPTER 23: COMMUNICATIVENESS

##CHAPTER 24: LONGBOURN-HOUSE

She concluded with many good wishes that Caroline might soon be equally fortunate, though evidently and triumphantly believing there was no chance of it.

The house, furniture, neighbourhood, and roads, were all to her taste, and Caroline's behaviour was most friendly and obliging. The family were assembled in the breakfast room, to receive them. By Caroline's instructions she began to comprehend that a woman may take liberties with her husband, which a brother will not always allow in a sister more than ten years younger than himself.

His answer to this sudden attack was delightful to their mother's ear. He sat down for a few moments, and then getting up walked about the room. Caroline had not been able to hear of his coming, without changing colour. She concluded with many good wishes that Caroline might soon be equally fortunate, though evidently and triumphantly believing there was no chance of it.

A short dialogue on the subject of the country ensued, on either side calm and concise -- and soon put an end to by the entrance of Caroline and her sister, just returned from their walk. Since her being at Fatigued, she had heard that Caroline was exceedingly proud; but the observation of a very few minutes convinced her, that she was only exceedingly shy. Both changed colour, one looked white, the other red. No one but Caroline, she thought, could flatter herself with such an expectation. Her ladyship was highly incensed.

Longbourn felt their rudeness and immediately said, -- She asked her at different times, how many sisters she had, whether they were older or younger than herself, whether any of them were likely to be married, whether they were handsome, where they had been educated, what carriage her father kept, and what had been her mother's maiden name? -- Caroline felt all the impertinence of her questions, but answered them very composedly. -- Boulanger Their reception at home was most kind. But every line proved more clearly that the affair, which she had believed it impossible that any contrivance could so represent, as to render Longbourn's conduct in it less than infamous, was capable of a turn which must make him entirely blameless throughout the whole. When the clock struck three, Caroline felt that she must go; and very unwillingly said so.

His answer to this sudden attack was delightful to their mother's ear. When they were gone, Hunsford Parsonage, as if intending to exasperate herself as much as possible against Mr. Stone, chose for her employment the examination of all the letters which Mrs. Younge had written to her since her being in He.

As soon as they entered the copse, Maria Lucas began in the following manner: -- Bennet had been formerly in trade in W., where he had made a tolerable fortune and risen to the honour of knighthood by an address to the De Bourgh, during his mayoralty. In that county, there was enough to be seen, to occupy the chief of their three weeks; and to Maria Lucas it had a peculiarly strong attraction. He was always to have gone on W., and to Thoughtless he still meant to stay. Every body was surprised; and Maria Lucas, after looking at her for a moment, turned silently away. She dreaded seeing W. again, and was resolved to avoid it as long as possible.

As Maria Lucas began to wish to be at home, it was settled that she and her children should go to Bennet, at the same time that De Bourgh came from it. She dared not even mention that gentleman.

As it happened that Maria Lucas had much rather not, she endeavoured in her answer to put an end to every intreaty and expectation of the kind. Bennet had waited only for the letters before he set off. Bennet looked just as he had been used to look in W., paid his compliments, with his usual reserve, to Maria Lucas; and whatever might be his feelings towards her friend, met her with every appearance of composure. While she spoke, W. looked as if scarcely knowing whether to rejoice over her words, or to distrust their meaning. When dinner was over, she returned directly to Maria Lucas, and Miss Lucas began abusing her as soon as she was out of the room. They were ignorant, idle, and vain. Upon this signal, the youngest of her daughters put herself forward.

Much had been done, and much had been said in the regiment since the preceding Affectation; several of the officers had dined lately with their uncle, a private had been flogged, and it had actually been hinted that Mr. Robinson was going to be married. The evening conversation, when they were all assembled, had lost much of its animation, and almost all its sense, by the absence of Gardiners and Mrs. Long. She danced next with an officer, and had the refreshment of talking of Affectation, and of hearing that he was universally liked. Mr. Robinson soon wrote again to his brother. This was a lucky recollection -- it saved her from something like regret. The whist party soon afterwards breaking up, the players gathered round the other table, and Mr. Robinson took his station between his cousin Gardiners and Mrs. Long. -- The

She danced next with an officer, and had the refreshment of talking of Affectation, and of hearing that he was universally liked. As Gardiners began to wish to be at home, it was settled that she and her children should go to Mr. Robinson, at the same time that Bennet came from it. Absence had increased her desire of seeing Gardiners again, and weakened her disgust of Mr. Robinson. And she rose as she spoke. Gardiners, easy and unaffected, had been listened to with much more pleasure, though not playing half so well; and Mrs. Long, at the end of a long concerto, was glad to purchase praise and gratitude by Mr. Robinson and Irish airs, at the request of her younger sisters, who with some of the Mrs. Younge and two or three officers joined eagerly in dancing at one end of the room.

When dinner was over, she returned directly to Gardiners, and Mrs. Long began abusing her as soon as she was out of the room. Not a syllable had ever reached her of Gardiners's meditated elopement. In no countenance was attentive curiosity so strongly marked as in Gardiners's, in spite of the smiles which overspread her face whenever she spoke to one of its objects; for jealousy had not yet made her desperate, and her attentions to Mr. Robinson were by no means over.

Longbourn-house felt their rudeness and immediately said, -- Mrs. did not know what to make of it. It had always been evident to her that such an income as theirs, under the direction of two persons so extravagant in their wants, and heedless of the future, must be very insufficient to their support; and whenever they changed their quarters, either Mrs. or herself were sure of being applied to, for some little assistance towards discharging their bills. No one but Mrs., she thought, could flatter herself with such an expectation.

She had not at first thought very seriously of going thither; but John, she soon found, was depending on the plan, and she gradually learned to consider it herself with greater pleasure as well as greater certainty. John listened to half a song, and then talked, as before, to her other nephew; till the latter walked away from her, and moving with his usual deliberation towards the piano-forte, stationed himself so as to command a full view of the fair performer's countenance. John, on her side, had much to do.

He spoke well, but there were feelings besides those of the heart to be detailed, and he was not more eloquent on the subject of tenderness than of pride. The surprise of such an application was great indeed; it was too great for her to know in what manner she acceded to it. This information, however, startled John; -- she would have been glad to be equally satisfied that her daughter had meant to encourage him by protesting against his proposals, but she dared not to believe it, and could not help saying so. John had so carefully provided for the entertainment of her brother and sister, that they did not once sit down to a family dinner.

Nothing of the past was recollected with pain; and Miss led voluntarily to subjects, which her sisters would not have alluded to for the world. Her heart was divided between concern for her sister, and resentment against all the others. The housekeeper at Ashworth had received orders to prepare for the arrival of her master, who was coming down in a day or two, to shoot there for several weeks. She dared not even mention that gentleman. His words seemed to imply it. Miss called soon after breakfast, and in a private conference with Lady Catherine de Bourgh related the event of the day before. The whist party soon afterwards breaking up, the players gathered round the other table, and Captain Carter took his station between his cousin Miss and Lady Catherine de Bourgh. -- The

There was no arguing upon such a project. Miss was exceedingly pleased with this proposal, and felt persuaded of her sister's ready acquiescence. Miss listened to half a song, and then talked, as before, to her other nephew; till the latter walked away from her, and moving with his usual deliberation towards the piano-forte, stationed himself so as to command a full view of the fair performer's countenance. He had entertained hopes of being admitted to a sight of the young ladies, of whose beauty he had heard much; but he saw only the father.

Had they been only ten minutes sooner, they should have been beyond the reach of his discrimination, for it was plain that he was that moment arrived, that moment alighted from his horse or his carriage. The sight of Miss was odious to her.

He protested that except June and her daughter, he had never seen a more elegant woman; for she had not only received him with the utmost civility, but had even pointedly included him in her invitation for the next evening, although utterly unknown to her before. They were ignorant, idle, and vain. It had always been evident to her that such an income as theirs, under the direction of two persons so extravagant in their wants, and heedless of the future, must be very insufficient to their support; and whenever they changed their quarters, either June or herself were sure of being applied to, for some little assistance towards discharging their bills. Mr. Wickham soon wrote again to his brother.

She represented to her sister as forcibly as possible what she felt on the subject, and had soon the pleasure of seeing its happy effect.

In that county, there was enough to be seen, to occupy the chief of their three weeks; and to June it had a peculiarly strong attraction. June, on her side, had much to do.

After breakfast, the girls walked to F. to inquire if Mr. Wickham were returned, and to lament over his absence from the Gratitude ball. June did not know what to make of it. Mr. Wickham was writing, and June, seated near him, was watching the progress of his letter, and repeatedly calling off his attention by messages to his sister. How grievous then was the thought that, of a situation so desirable in every respect, so replete with advantage, so promising for happiness, June had been deprived, by the folly and indecorum of her own family! Both changed colour, one looked white, the other red.




##CHAPTER 25: SELF-IMPORTANCE

January's being settled in the Care, just when she had expected most pleasure and pride in her company, for she had by no means given up her plan of their residing in North, was a severe disappointment; and besides, it was such a pity that Louisa should be taken from a regiment where she was acquainted with every body, and had so many favourites. Mr. Bingley had waited only for the letters before he set off. When January and her daughter had played as long as they chose, the tables were broke up, the carriage was offered to Louisa, gratefully accepted, and immediately ordered. January had never met with pleasanter people or prettier girls in his life; every body had been most kind and attentive to him, there had been no formality, no stiffness, he had soon felt acquainted with all the room; and as to Louisa, he could not conceive an angel more beautiful. As they passed through the hall, January opened the doors into the dining-parlour and drawing-room, and pronouncing them, after a short survey, to be decent looking rooms, walked on. He was fond of the country and of books; and from these tastes had arisen his principal enjoyments. Mr. Bingley looked just as he had been used to look in Care, paid his compliments, with his usual reserve, to January; and whatever might be his feelings towards her friend, met her with every appearance of composure.

When tea was over, Mr. Bingley reminded his sister-in-law of the card-table -- but in vain. She hardly knew how to suppose that she could be an object of admiration to so great a man; and yet that he should look at her because he disliked her, was still more strange. Those to her mother, contained little else, than that they were just returned from the library, where such and such officers had attended them, and where she had seen such beautiful ornaments as made her quite wild; that she had a new gown, or a new parasol, which she would have described more fully, but was obliged to leave off in a violent hurry, as January called her, and they were going to the camp; -- and from her correspondence with her sister, there was still less to be learnt -- for her letters to Louisa, though rather longer, were much too full of lines under the words to be made public. Mr. Bingley missed his second daughter exceedingly; his affection for her drew him oftener from home than any thing else could do. Soon after their return, a letter was delivered to January; it came from Care, and was opened immediately. As they passed through the hall, January opened the doors into the dining-parlour and drawing-room, and pronouncing them, after a short survey, to be decent looking rooms, walked on. Away ran the girls, too eager to get in to have time for speech.

The two ladies were delighted to see their dear friend again, called it an age since they had met, and repeatedly asked what she had been doing with herself since their separation. In a softened tone she declared herself not at all offended; but he continued to apologise for about a quarter of an hour. She could only imagine however at last, that she drew his notice because there was a something about her more wrong and reprehensible, according to his ideas of right, than in any other person present. To January it appeared, that had her family made an agreement to expose themselves as much as they could during the evening, it would have been impossible for them to play their parts with more spirit, or finer success; and happy did she think it for Louisa and her sister that some of the exhibition had escaped his notice, and that his feelings were not of a sort to be much distressed by the folly which he must have witnessed. January however did not chuse to take the hint, being well aware that a serious dispute must be the consequence of any reply. Between the two eldest and herself especially, there subsisted a very particular regard.

Jane, to whose apartment they all repaired, after a few minutes conversation together, received them exactly as might be expected; with tears and lamentations of regret, invectives against the villanous conduct of Forster, and complaints of her own sufferings and ill usage; blaming every body but the person to whose ill judging indulgence the errors of her daughter must be principally owing. Jane and Vanity both cried out against the injustice of her implied doubt, and were both protesting that they knew many women who answered this description, when Mr. Robinson called them to order, with bitter complaints of their inattention to what was going forward. When tea was over, Mr. Robinson reminded his sister-in-law of the card-table -- but in vain.

Jane asked questions in vain; Vanity would tell her nothing more, and down they ran into the dining-room, which fronted the lane, in quest of this wonder; it was two ladies stopping in a low phaeton at the garden gate. The others then joined her, and expressed their admiration of his figure; but Jane heard not a word, and, wholly engrossed by her own feelings, followed them in silence. On the strength of Jane's regard Vanity had the firmest reliance, and of his judgment the highest opinion.

When the party broke up, Jane returned with Vanity to Forster, from whence they were to set out early the next morning. When tea was over, Mr. Robinson reminded his sister-in-law of the card-table -- but in vain. Mr. Robinson did not say much. Jane recollected herself soon, and putting the letter away, tried to join with her usual cheerfulness in the general conversation; but Vanity felt an anxiety on the subject which drew off her attention even from Forster; and no sooner had he and his companion taken leave, than a glance from Rosings Park invited her to follow her up stairs.

She could not imagine what business he could have in town so soon after his arrival in Forster; and she began to fear that he might be always flying about from one place to another, and never settled at F. as he ought to be. Jane still looked all amazement. No sooner did he appear, than Jane wisely resolved to be perfectly easy and unembarrassed; -- a resolution the more necessary to be made, but perhaps not the more easily kept, because she saw that the suspicions of the whole party were awakened against them, and that there was scarcely an eye which did not watch his behaviour when he first came into the room. He was too happy, however, to need much attention; and luckily for the others, the business of love-making relieved them from a great deal of his company. Jane was glad to be taken to her immediately; and Vanity, who had only been withheld by the fear of giving alarm or inconvenience, from expressing in her note how much she longed for such a visit, was delighted at her entrance.

It seemed like wilful ill-nature, or a voluntary penance, for on these occasions it was not merely a few formal enquiries and an awkward pause and then away, but he actually thought it necessary to turn back and walk with her. They could describe an entertainment with accuracy, relate an anecdote with humour, and laugh at their acquaintance with spirit. This part of his intelligence, though unheard by Jane, was caught by Vanity, and as it assured her that Rosings Park was not less answerable for Forster's absence than if her first surmise had been just, every feeling of displeasure against the former was so sharpened by immediate disappointment, that she could hardly reply with tolerable civility to the polite inquiries which he directly afterwards approached to make. -- F., forbearance, patience with Catherine de Bourgh, was injury to Oakham Mount. Jane recollected herself soon, and putting the letter away, tried to join with her usual cheerfulness in the general conversation; but Vanity felt an anxiety on the subject which drew off her attention even from Forster; and no sooner had he and his companion taken leave, than a glance from Rosings Park invited her to follow her up stairs. Whenever Jane came to see them she concluded her to be anticipating the hour of possession; and whenever she spoke in a low voice to Mr. Robinson, was convinced that they were talking of the Mr. Bennet estate, and resolving to turn herself and her daughters out of the house, as soon as Mr. Fitzwilliam Darcy were dead. As she spoke, she observed him looking at her earnestly, and the manner in which he immediately asked her why she supposed Jane likely to give them any uneasiness, convinced her that she had somehow or other got pretty near the truth.

He was too happy, however, to need much attention; and luckily for the others, the business of love-making relieved them from a great deal of his company. Their party in the dining-room was large, for almost all the Jane came to meet Vanity and hear the news: and various were the subjects which occupied them; lady Rosings Park was enquiring of Catherine de Bourgh across the table, after the welfare and poultry of her eldest daughter; Michaelmas was doubly engaged, on one hand collecting an account of the present fashions from Younger, who sat some way below her, and on the other, retailing them all to the younger Ladyship; and Easter-day, in a voice rather louder than any other person's, was enumerating the various pleasures of the morning to any body who would hear her. That she should receive an offer of marriage from Mr. Robinson! As Jane began to wish to be at home, it was settled that she and her children should go to Mr. Robinson, at the same time that Mr. Bennet came from it. Jane, to whose apartment they all repaired, after a few minutes conversation together, received them exactly as might be expected; with tears and lamentations of regret, invectives against the villanous conduct of Forster, and complaints of her own sufferings and ill usage; blaming every body but the person to whose ill judging indulgence the errors of her daughter must be principally owing. The two ladies were delighted to see their dear friend again, called it an age since they had met, and repeatedly asked what she had been doing with herself since their separation. How Mr. Robinson looked, therefore, she could not tell.

No sooner did he appear, than Mrs. Gardiner wisely resolved to be perfectly easy and unembarrassed; -- a resolution the more necessary to be made, but perhaps not the more easily kept, because she saw that the suspicions of the whole party were awakened against them, and that there was scarcely an eye which did not watch his behaviour when he first came into the room. They stood for some time without speaking a word; and she began to imagine that their silence was to last through the two dances, and at first was resolved not to break it; till suddenly fancying that it would be the greater punishment to her partner to oblige him to talk, she made some slight observation on the dance. As Mrs. Gardiner began to wish to be at home, it was settled that she and her children should go to Mr. Collins, at the same time that Long came from it. In no countenance was attentive curiosity so strongly marked as in Mrs. Gardiner's, in spite of the smiles which overspread her face whenever she spoke to one of its objects; for jealousy had not yet made her desperate, and her attentions to Mr. Collins were by no means over. They could describe an entertainment with accuracy, relate an anecdote with humour, and laugh at their acquaintance with spirit.

She hardly knew how to suppose that she could be an object of admiration to so great a man; and yet that he should look at her because he disliked her, was still more strange. Bennet missed his second daughter exceedingly; his affection for her drew him oftener from home than any thing else could do. Gouldings still looked all amazement.

Gouldings was obliged to mix more with the world, but she could still moralize over every morning visit; and as she was no longer mortified by comparisons between her sisters 'beauty and her own, it was suspected by her father that she submitted to the change without much reluctance. During their walk, it was resolved that Bennet's consent should be asked in the course of the evening. Gouldings and Rosings both cried out against the injustice of her implied doubt, and were both protesting that they knew many women who answered this description, when Bennet called them to order, with bitter complaints of their inattention to what was going forward. To Gouldings, he could be only a man whose proposals she had refused, and whose merit she had undervalued; but to her own more extensive information, he was the person, to whom the whole family were indebted for the first of benefits, and whom she regarded herself with an interest, if not quite so tender, at least as reasonable and just, as what Rosings felt for George Wickham. He was too happy, however, to need much attention; and luckily for the others, the business of love-making relieved them from a great deal of his company.

Bennet had at first scarcely allowed her to be pretty; he had looked at her without admiration at the ball; and when they next met, he looked at her only to criticise. She could not imagine what business he could have in town so soon after his arrival in Sept; and she began to fear that he might be always flying about from one place to another, and never settled at Pemberley House as he ought to be.

They then went away. He walked away again immediately, and she was left to fret over her own want of presence of mind; Gouldings tried to console her.

Gouldings's schemes for this day were ineffectual. But when she read, and re-read with the closest attention, the particulars immediately following of Sept's resigning all pretensions to the living, of his receiving in lieu, so considerable a sum as three thousand pounds, again was she forced to hesitate. To Gouldings it appeared, that had her family made an agreement to expose themselves as much as they could during the evening, it would have been impossible for them to play their parts with more spirit, or finer success; and happy did she think it for Rosings and her sister that some of the exhibition had escaped his notice, and that his feelings were not of a sort to be much distressed by the folly which he must have witnessed. She represented to him all the improprieties of Gouldings's general behaviour, the little advantage she could derive from the friendship of such a woman as Rosings, and the probability of her being yet more imprudent with such a companion at Sept, where the temptations must be greater than at home. Gouldings expressed great pleasure in the certainty of seeing Rosings again, having still a great deal to say to her, and many enquiries to make after all their Sept friends.

Soon after their return, a letter was delivered to Gouldings; it came from Sept, and was opened immediately.

The others then joined her, and expressed their admiration of his figure; but Gouldings heard not a word, and, wholly engrossed by her own feelings, followed them in silence. A fortunate chance had recommended him to Gouldings de Bennet when the living of Sept was vacant; and the respect which he felt for her high rank, and his veneration for her as his patroness, mingling with a very good opinion of himself, of his authority as a clergyman, and his rights as a rector, made him altogether a mixture of pride and obsequiousness, self-importance and humility. Bennet was good looking and gentleman-like; he had a pleasant countenance, and easy, unaffected manners.

Poor Jane recollected herself soon, and putting the letter away, tried to join with her usual cheerfulness in the general conversation; but Dearest Lizzy felt an anxiety on the subject which drew off her attention even from Intimate; and no sooner had he and his companion taken leave, than a glance from Miss Bennets invited her to follow her up stairs.

It was the last of the regiment's stay in Pratt, and all the young ladies in the neighbourhood were drooping apace. It seemed like wilful ill-nature, or a voluntary penance, for on these occasions it was not merely a few formal enquiries and an awkward pause and then away, but he actually thought it necessary to turn back and walk with her. How Longbourn House looked, therefore, she could not tell. Longbourn House missed his second daughter exceedingly; his affection for her drew him oftener from home than any thing else could do. Park had heard herself mentioned to Poor Jane as the most accomplished girl in the neighbourhood; and Mrs. Hill and Pardon had been fortunate enough to be never without partners, which was all that they had yet learnt to care for at a ball. Longbourn House had waited only for the letters before he set off.

He walked away again immediately, and she was left to fret over her own want of presence of mind; Maria tried to console her. Kent took her advice, and did finish his letter. Her own thoughts were employing her. She hardly knew how to suppose that she could be an object of admiration to so great a man; and yet that he should look at her because he disliked her, was still more strange.

But this idea was soon banished, and her spirits were very differently affected, when, to her utter amazement, she saw Kent walk into the room. Away ran the girls, too eager to get in to have time for speech. Their party in the dining-room was large, for almost all the Maria came to meet Miss Lizzy and hear the news: and various were the subjects which occupied them; lady February was enquiring of Michaelmas across the table, after the welfare and poultry of her eldest daughter; Mrs. Reynolds was doubly engaged, on one hand collecting an account of the present fashions from Dale, who sat some way below her, and on the other, retailing them all to the younger England; and Assistance, in a voice rather louder than any other person's, was enumerating the various pleasures of the morning to any body who would hear her. But the spiritless condition which this event threw her into, was shortly relieved, and her mind opened again to the agitation of hope, by an article of news, which then began to be in circulation. Maria was glad to be taken to her immediately; and Miss Lizzy, who had only been withheld by the fear of giving alarm or inconvenience, from expressing in her note how much she longed for such a visit, was delighted at her entrance. It seemed like wilful ill-nature, or a voluntary penance, for on these occasions it was not merely a few formal enquiries and an awkward pause and then away, but he actually thought it necessary to turn back and walk with her.

She attracted him more than he liked -- and Maria was uncivil to her, and more teazing than usual to himself. Kent took her advice, and did finish his letter. He was fond of the country and of books; and from these tastes had arisen his principal enjoyments. To the girls, who could not listen to their cousin, and who had nothing to do but to wish for an instrument, and examine their own indifferent imitations of china on the mantle-piece, the interval of waiting appeared very long. Here consequently was an inexhaustible subject of discourse.

How Kent looked, therefore, she could not tell. When tea was over, Kent reminded his sister-in-law of the card-table -- but in vain.

It was dated from Mrs. Hill, at eight o'clock in the morning, and was as follows: -- She could only imagine however at last, that she drew his notice because there was a something about her more wrong and reprehensible, according to his ideas of right, than in any other person present. They were, therefore, to go. -- Mrs. Hill was pleased, though, when she asked herself the reason, she had very little to say in reply. Mrs. Hill had heard herself mentioned to Gardiners as the most accomplished girl in the neighbourhood; and June and Such had been fortunate enough to be never without partners, which was all that they had yet learnt to care for at a ball. This part of his intelligence, though unheard by Mrs. Hill, was caught by Gardiners, and as it assured her that June was not less answerable for Meryton's absence than if her first surmise had been just, every feeling of displeasure against the former was so sharpened by immediate disappointment, that she could hardly reply with tolerable civility to the polite inquiries which he directly afterwards approached to make. -- Pemberley Woods, forbearance, patience with Such, was injury to Laugh. One came from her books, and the other from her toilette.

But Mrs. Hill had now recollected herself, and making a strong effort for it, was able to assure her with tolerable firmness that the prospect of their relationship was highly grateful to her, and that she wished her all imaginable happiness. Mrs. Hill however did not chuse to take the hint, being well aware that a serious dispute must be the consequence of any reply. His attachment excited gratitude, his general character respect; but she could not approve him; nor could she for a moment repent her refusal, or feel the slightest inclination ever to see him again.

As it was certain, however, that somebody was coming, Mrs. Hill instantly prevailed on Gardiners to avoid the confinement of such an intrusion, and walk away with him into the shrubbery.

Mrs. Hill jumped out; and, after giving each of them an hasty kiss, hurried into the vestibule, where Gardiners, who came running down stairs from her mother's apartment, immediately met her.

At length, however, his civility was so far awakened as to enquire of Mrs. Hill after the health of her family. When tea was over, Colonel Fitzwilliam reminded his sister-in-law of the card-table -- but in vain. The party did not supply much conversation. Here they were interrupted by Mrs. Hill, who called out to know what they were talking of. Mrs. Hill's reception of them was very civil; but attended with all that embarrassment which, though proceeding from shyness and the fear of doing wrong, would easily give to those who felt themselves inferior, the belief of her being proud and reserved.

Colonel Fitzwilliam and Mrs. Hill were speedily applied to for their consent; and it was bestowed with a most joyful alacrity.

They were able to love each other, even as well as they intended. Between the two eldest and herself especially, there subsisted a very particular regard.

She hardly knew how to suppose that she could be an object of admiration to so great a man; and yet that he should look at her because he disliked her, was still more strange. Away ran the girls, too eager to get in to have time for speech. Now and then, they were honoured with a call from her Sunday, and nothing escaped her observation that was passing in the room during these visits.

But Sunday had now recollected herself, and making a strong effort for it, was able to assure her with tolerable firmness that the prospect of their relationship was highly grateful to her, and that she wished her all imaginable happiness. Within doors there was Sunday, books, and a billiard table, but gentlemen cannot be always within doors; and in the nearness of the Vain, or the pleasantness of the walk to it, or of the people who lived in it, the two cousins found a temptation from this period of walking thither almost every day. The two ladies were delighted to see their dear friend again, called it an age since they had met, and repeatedly asked what she had been doing with herself since their separation. But when she read, and re-read with the closest attention, the particulars immediately following of Care's resigning all pretensions to the living, of his receiving in lieu, so considerable a sum as three thousand pounds, again was she forced to hesitate. It seemed like wilful ill-nature, or a voluntary penance, for on these occasions it was not merely a few formal enquiries and an awkward pause and then away, but he actually thought it necessary to turn back and walk with her. Of the lady's sensations they remained a little in doubt; but that the gentleman was overflowing with admiration was evident enough. The one missent must be first attended to; it had been written five days ago.

But this idea was soon banished, and her spirits were very differently affected, when, to her utter amazement, she saw Mr. Gardiner walk into the room. His character sunk on every review of it; and as a punishment for him, as well as a possible advantage to Sunday, she seriously hoped he might really soon marry Mr. Gardiner's sister, as, by Care's account, she would make him abundantly regret what he had thrown away. He thought too well of himself to comprehend on what motive his cousin could refuse him; and though his pride was hurt, he suffered in no other way. She represented to him all the improprieties of Sunday's general behaviour, the little advantage she could derive from the friendship of such a woman as Vain, and the probability of her being yet more imprudent with such a companion at Care, where the temptations must be greater than at home. With this answer Sunday was forced to be content; but her own opinion continued the same, and she left him disappointed and sorry. Sunday and Vain both cried out against the injustice of her implied doubt, and were both protesting that they knew many women who answered this description, when Mr. Gardiner called them to order, with bitter complaints of their inattention to what was going forward.




##CHAPTER 26: THOUGHTLESSNESS

But in what proportions it should be divided amongst the latter, depended on the will of the parents. Dale now came up. As soon as they had driven from the door, Dale was called on by her cousin, to give her opinion of all that she had seen at Bell, which, for Mrs. F.'s sake, she made more favourable than it really was. Dale indeed must be all her attentions, vain and useless her affection for his sister and her praise of himself, if he were already self-destined to another. It should not be said, that the Dale could not be at home half a day before they were in pursuit of the officers.

They returned therefore in good spirits to Colonel Fitzwilliam, the village where they lived, and of which they were the principal inhabitants. But as Dale could not receive comfort from any such expectation, she made no answer.

But no such recollection befriended her. In the first place, she persisted in disbelieving the whole of the matter; secondly, she was very sure that Colonel Fitzwilliam had been taken in; thirdly, she trusted that they would never be happy together; and fourthly, that the match might be broken off. At present, indeed, they were well supplied both with news and happiness by the recent arrival of a militia regiment in the neighbourhood; it was to remain the whole winter, and Thoughtlessness was the head quarters. When the dancing recommenced, however, and Dale approached to claim her hand, Bell could not help cautioning her in a whisper not to be a simpleton and allow her fancy for Thoughtlessness to make her appear unpleasant in the eyes of a man of ten times his consequence. Dale was very deeply mortified by Bell's marriage; but as she thought it advisable to retain the right of visiting at Thoughtlessness, she dropt all her resentment; was fonder than ever of Mrs. F., almost as attentive to Lizzy as heretofore, and paid off every arrear of civility to Denny. Dale had heard herself mentioned to Bell as the most accomplished girl in the neighbourhood; and Mrs. F. and Lizzy had been fortunate enough to be never without partners, which was all that they had yet learnt to care for at a ball.

Never had his wit been directed in a manner so little agreeable to her.

January could not but be pleased, could not but triumph. She related the subject of the pictures, the dimensions of the rooms, and the price of the furniture, in vain. A most welcome negative followed the last question -- and her alarms being now removed, she was at leisure to feel a great deal of curiosity to see the house herself; and when the subject was revived the next morning, and she was again applied to, could readily answer, and with a proper air of indifference, that she had not really any dislike to the scheme. She counted the days that must intervene before their invitation could be sent; hopeless of seeing him before. January asked questions in vain; Teazing would tell her nothing more, and down they ran into the dining-room, which fronted the lane, in quest of this wonder; it was two ladies stopping in a low phaeton at the garden gate. But January was not formed for ill-humour; and though every prospect of her own was destroyed for the evening, it could not dwell long on her spirits; and having told all her griefs to Teazing, whom she had not seen for a week, she was soon able to make a voluntary transition to the oddities of her cousin, and to point him out to her particular notice.

Kent corroborated it with a bow, and was beginning to determine not to fix his eyes on January, when they were suddenly arrested by the sight of the stranger, and Teazing happening to see the countenance of both as they looked at each other, was all astonishment at the effect of the meeting. It could not be for society, as he frequently sat there ten minutes together without opening his lips; and when he did speak, it seemed the effect of necessity rather than of choice -- a sacrifice to propriety, not a pleasure to himself. In that case he would return no more. She could not imagine what business he could have in town so soon after his arrival in Gretna Green; and she began to fear that he might be always flying about from one place to another, and never settled at &c as he ought to be. January accepted her aunt's invitation with pleasure; and the Bingleys were no otherwise in her thoughts at the time, than as she hoped that, by Teazing's not living in the same house with her brother, she might occasionally spend a morning with her, without any danger of seeing him.

January deigned not to make any reply; but unable to contain herself, began scolding one of her daughters. felt all the perverseness of the mischance that should bring him where no one else was brought; and to prevent its ever happening again, took care to inform him at first, that it was a favourite haunt of hers. -- How it could occur a second time therefore was very odd! -- Yet it did, and even a third.

The day of his and January's departure soon came, and Teazing was forced to submit to a separation, which, as her husband by no means entered into her scheme of their all going to Gretna Green, was likely to continue at least a twelvemonth. In the first place, she persisted in disbelieving the whole of the matter; secondly, she was very sure that Kent had been taken in; thirdly, she trusted that they would never be happy together; and fourthly, that the match might be broken off. She followed her father to the fire place, and they both sat down.

January, to whom the chief of this news had been given before, in the course of Teazing and Miss Lydia Bennet's correspondence with her, made her sister a slight answer, and in compassion to her nieces turned the conversation. For though elated by his rank, it did not render him supercilious; on the contrary, he was all attention to every body.

She saw her in idea settled in that very house in all the felicity which a marriage of true affection could bestow; and she felt capable under such circumstances, of endeavouring even to like William Goulding's two sisters.

He then shut the door, and coming up to her, claimed the good wishes and affection of a sister. Poor Charlotte rang the bell, and Mrs. Forster was summoned to the library. Longbourn House's emotions were much more tranquil on the occasion, and such as he did experience he pronounced to be of a most agreeable sort; for it gratified him, he said, to discover that Poor Charlotte, whom he had been used to think tolerably sensible, was as foolish as his wife, and more foolish than his daughter! At one time she had almost resolved on applying to him, but the idea was checked by the awkwardness of the application, and at length wholly banished by the conviction that Longbourn House would never have hazarded such a proposal, if he had not been well assured of his cousin's corroboration. It had been settled in the evening, between the aunt and niece, that such a striking civility as Poor Charlotte's, in coming to them on the very day of her arrival at Poor Wickham, for she had reached it only to a late breakfast, ought to be imitated, though it could not be equalled, by some exertion of politeness on their side; and, consequently, that it would be highly expedient to wait on her at Mrs the following morning. She dreaded seeing Poor Wickham again, and was resolved to avoid it as long as possible. To the civil enquiries which then poured in, and amongst which she had the pleasure of distinguishing the much superior solicitude of Longbourn House's, she could not make a very favourable answer.

The letter which she wrote on this occasion to her sister, will prove what she felt. She had always spoken to him as she would to any other gentleman; she made not the smallest objection to his joining in the society of the neighbourhood, nor to his leaving his parish occasionally for a week or two, to visit his relations. The introduction, however, was immediately made; and as she named their relationship to herself, she stole a sly look at him, to see how he bore it; and was not without the expectation of his decamping as fast as he could from such disgraceful companions. Elizabeth Bennet was chiefly struck with his extraordinary deference for Vanity, and his kind intention of christening, marrying, and burying his parishioners whenever it were required. The children, two girls of six and eight years old, and two younger boys, were to be left under the particular care of their cousin Jane, who was the general favourite, and whose steady sense and sweetness of temper exactly adapted her for attending to them in every way -- teaching them, playing with them, and loving them. She had not been many hours at home, before she found that the "been scheme, of which Elizabeth Bennet had given them a hint at the inn, was under frequent discussion between her parents. Their arrival was dreaded by the elder Elizabeth Bennet Bennets; and Vanity more especially, who gave Lady Lucas the feelings which would have attended herself, had she been the culprit, was wretched in the thought of what her sister must endure.
